## Domain Knowledge (LLM-generated)
**Domain**: thermomechanical constitutive modeling (materials science)
**Analysis**: This task models the stress-strain-temperature relationship of a material, which is central to constitutive modeling in materials science. Temperature typically softens materials (reducing modulus and flow stress), while strain hardening increases stress with deformation.

**Common formula structures in this domain**:
- Hooke's law (temperature-dependent): sigma = c0 * epsilon (linear elasticity at fixed T)
- Temperature-dependent elastic modulus: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon (linear modulus variation with T)
- Hollomon power-law hardening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 (strain hardening without temperature)
- Power-law with thermal softening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * (c2 + c3 * T) (combined hardening and linear thermal softening)
- Johnson-Cook type: sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2) * (1 + c3 * T) (additive yield + hardening with thermal factor)
- Exponential thermal softening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * exp(c2 * T) (Arrhenius-like temperature dependence)
- Voce hardening with temperature: sigma = (c0 + c1 * (1 - exp(c2 * epsilon))) * (c3 + c4 * T) (saturating hardening with thermal softening)
- Ramberg-Osgood inverse form: sigma = c0 * epsilon * (1 + c1 * epsilon**c2)**(-1) (nonlinear with saturation)
- Polynomial stress-strain: sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * epsilon**2 + c2 * epsilon**3 (polynomial constitutive law)
- Thermal activation model: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * exp(c2 * (c3 * T)**(-1)) (thermally activated flow stress)
- Ludwik model with temperature: sigma = c0 * (c1 + c2 * T) + c3 * epsilon**c4 (yield stress + hardening, T-dependent yield)
- Swift hardening: sigma = c0 * (epsilon + c1)**c2 * (c3 + c4 * T) (shifted power law with thermal factor)
- Hyperbolic sine flow: sigma = c0 * T * asinh(c1 * epsilon * exp(c2 * (c3 * T)**(-1))) (Garofalo/Sellars-Tegart creep model)
- Bilinear model: sigma = c0 * epsilon * (c1 + c2 * T) + c3 * epsilon**2 * (c4 + c5 * T) (temperature-dependent bilinear response)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Thermal softening in metals typically appears as a multiplicative factor (1 - c*T) or (1 - (c*T)**m) applied to the mechanical stress term
- Strain hardening in polycrystalline metals often follows power-law form epsilon**n with n between 0.1 and 0.5
- Thermally activated processes (creep, dislocation motion) introduce inverse temperature in exponential: exp(c / T)
- Saturation hardening (Voce-type) uses (1 - exp(-c*epsilon)) to capture dislocation density saturation
- Additive decomposition of yield stress and hardening stress: sigma = sigma_y(T) + H(epsilon, T) separates elastic limit from plastic flow
- At high homologous temperatures, stress may scale as T * f(epsilon/T) reflecting entropy-dominated elasticity (e.g., rubber elasticity)
- Phase transitions or material instabilities can introduce non-monotonic temperature dependence, requiring polynomial or piecewise T terms
- Cross-term epsilon*T often captures the physical effect that hardening rate itself is temperature-dependent
